Consequences of the Husband's Admission After Li'an
Explaining the legal effects when the husband admits to lying after completing the imprecatory oath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on lineage and hadd when the husband admits after li'an, and distinguish Imam Abu Hanifa's view from that of the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A husband's admission after li'an re-establishes lineage and triggers hadd for the implicit accusation.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explains what happens when a husband, after completing li'an, admits that he lied. The ruling depends on whether the admission is explicit or implicit, and it involves both lineage and the hadd punishment for qadhf. There is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two disciples (Sahibayn) that we will analyze.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is 'ikdhab'? It is the husband's admission that he lied in his accusation during li'an. This can be explicit (verbal) or implicit (e.g., claiming the child after denial). The ruling under Imam Abu Hanifa is that the child's lineage is re-established and the husband is liable for hadd (80 lashes for qadhf).
The hadd is for the implicit accusation in the li'an, not the original accusation.
2
Locate the dalil
The passage draws an analogy (qiyas) to witnesses of zina who retract their testimony. When they retract, they are punished with hadd for the accusation implied in their testimony. Similarly, the husband's li'an contains an implicit accusation, and his admission (retraction) triggers hadd.
This is a dalil naqli (textual proof) based on a hadith about li'an, though not explicitly quoted.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The legal link: In li'an, the husband accuses his wife of zina under oath. When he later admits he lied, that admission reveals that his original accusation was false, constituting a new act of qadhf. This new accusation is analogous to the accusation contained in witness testimony. Thus, the hadd becomes wajib.
The distinction is between the first accusation (expiated by li'an) and the second implicit accusation (exposed by admission).
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree with Imam Abu Hanifa. They hold that admission after li'an does not re-establish lineage and does not impose hadd, because the li'an already settled the matter and the admission is not a new legal act.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf with practical consequences for inheritance and punishment.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Imam Abu Hanifa, as preferred by Al-Marghinani in Fath al-Qadir. The reason is that the analogy to retracting witnesses is strong, and the admission creates a fresh legal effect.
For lineage, the living heir's need for lineage is also a factor (see the case of the child's child).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وشمل الإكذاب صريحاً وضمناً ولهذا لو مات الولد المنفي عن مال فادعى الملاعن لا يثبت نسبه ويحد، فإن كان قد ترك ولداً ثبت نسبه من الأب وورثه الأب لاحتياج الحي إلى النسب، ولو ترك بنتاً ولها ابن فأكذب الملاعن نفسه يثبت نسب الولد منه عند الإمام خلافاً لهما؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وظاهر ما في الكتاب أن الإكذاب بعد اللعان ووجوب الحد عليه ليس باعتبار قذفه الأول لأنه أخذ بموجبه وهو اللعان بل باعتبار القذف الثاني الذي تضمنه كلمات اللعان كشهود الزنا إذا رجعوا فإنهم يحدون باعتبار ما تضمنته شهادتهم من القذف. أما إذا أكذب نفسه قبل اللعان ينظر؛ فإن لم يطلقها قبل الإكذاب حد أيضاً، وإن أبانها ثم أكذب نفسه فلا حد ولا لعان لأن اللعان أثره التفريق بينهما وهو لا يتأتى بعد البينونة لحصوله بالإبانة وهو لا يصح بدون حكمه ولا يجب الحد لأن قذفه وقع موجباً للعان فلا ينقلب موجباً للحد.
And [the term] 'admission' includes explicit and implicit [admission]. For this reason, if the denied child dies leaving property and the husband [who performed li'an] claims [the child], his lineage is not established and he is punished with hadd. But if the child left a child [of his own], his lineage is established from the father and the father inherits from him because the living one needs lineage. If the child left a daughter and she has a son, and the husband admits his lying, the lineage of the child is established from him according to Imam Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two [disciples]; as in Fath al-Qadir. What is apparent from the book is that the admission after li'an and the obligation of hadd upon him is not on account of his first accusation because he acted upon its requirement which is li'an, but rather on account of the second accusation that is contained in the words of li'an, like the witnesses of zina when they retract – they are punished with hadd on account of what their testimony contained of accusation. As for if he admits before li'an, consideration is made: if he had not divorced her before the admission, he is also punished with hadd. And if he had separated her irrevocably (abana) then admits, there is no hadd and no li'an because the effect of li'an is separation between them, which cannot occur after irrevocable separation since it is achieved by the irrevocable divorce, and it is not valid without [the judge's] ruling, and hadd is not obligatory because his accusation fell as necessitating li'an, so it cannot turn into necessitating hadd.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the ruling of the husband's admission (ikdhab) after completing li'an. The commentary explains that 'ikdhab' includes both explicit verbal admission and implicit admission (e.g., claiming the child after denial). If the husband admits after li'an, the child's lineage is re-established according to Imam Abu Hanifa (contrary to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and the husband becomes liable for the hadd punishment for false accusation (qadhf). However, this hadd is not for the original accusation (which was already expiated by li'an) but for the implicit accusation contained in the words of li'an—similar to witnesses of zina who retract; they receive hadd for the accusation implied in their testimony. If the husband admits before li'an, the ruling depends on whether he had divorced her: if not yet divorced, he receives hadd; if he had already issued an irrevocable divorce (ba'in), then neither li'an nor hadd apply, because the purpose of li'an (separation) is already fulfilled, and the original accusation was for li'an, not for hadd.

Key Terms
الإكذاب (al-ikdhab): The husband's admission that he lied in his accusation during li'an.
الحد (al-hadd): The fixed punishment for false accusation of zina (qadhf), which is 80 lashes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately after completing li'an, regardless of admission
B) Only if he explicitly admits to lying after li'an
C) Never, because li'an removes the hadd
D) Only if the wife also admits

Key Takeaway
The husband's admission (ikdhab) after li'an re-establishes lineage and imposes hadd for the implicit accusation, per Imam Abu Hanifa.

Nature of Separation in Li'an: Divorce or Permanent Prohibition
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the nature of li'an separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between the opinions of Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the permanence of separation in li'an and evaluate the evidence (dalil) for each position.

Core Idea
Li'an separation is temporary; remarriage is possible after admission, contrary to Abu Yusuf's view.

This lesson delves into a critical intra-Hanafi dispute over the nature of the separation (furqa) resulting from li'an. Understanding this ikhtilaf clarifies how the Hanafi school handles cases where spouses accuse each other of adultery and then later admit falsehood. The practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) directly affects whether the couple can reunite.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
State the core disagreement: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad hold that the separation is a temporary prohibition that ends upon admission (ikdhab), while Abu Yusuf holds it is a permanent prohibition like that of fosterage.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not inter-madhhab.
2
Examine Abu Yusuf's proof
Analyze his evidence: the hadith 'al-mutala'inian la yajtami'an abadan' (the two who perform li'an will never be together) and the sunnah of 'Uwaymir who divorced irrevocably after li'an.
Abu Yusuf interprets the hadith literally as a permanent ban.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Understand how Abu Hanifa and Muhammad respond: the hadith is figurative (majaz), referring only while li'an is in effect; after admission, the legal effect disappears, so remarriage is allowed. They analogize to the Quranic verse about the People of the Cave (18:20) where 'never' is conditional.
The response uses istihsan and qiyas.
4
Compare and conclude
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: under Abu Hanifa, spouses can remarry after admission; under Abu Yusuf, they cannot. The mufta bihi is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
Tarjih is based on the strength of the evidence and the principle of alleviating hardship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالحاصل أن الفرقة باللعان يزول بها ملك النكاح وتوجب حرمة الاجتماع والتزوج ما داما على حال اللعان فإن أكذب أحدهما نفسه جاز التناكح والاجتماع عند الإمام والثالث. وقال الثاني : إنها توجب حرمة مؤبدة كحرمة الرضاع ... والمصاهرة لقوله عليه السلام والمتلاعنان لا يجتمعان أبداً ويقتضي قوله أن الفرقة لا تتوقف على القضاء كما أشار إليه في فتح القدير. ولهما أن عويمراً طلق الملاعنة ثلاثاً فصار سنة المتلاعنين لأنه يجب عليه أن يطلقها فإن لم يفعل ناب القاضي منابه كما في العنين فكانت الفرقة طلاقاً، وأما الحديث فلا يمكن العمل بحقيقته لأن حقيقة المتفاعل المتشاغل بالفعل ولما فرغا منه زالت الحقيقة فانصرف المراد إلى الحكم وهو أن يكون حكمه باقياً وبعد الإكذاب لم يبق حكمه لبطلانه فلم يبق حقيقة ولا حكماً فجاز اجتماعهما ونظيره قوله تعالى في قصة أصحاب الكهف إنهم إن يظهروا عليكم يرجموكم أو يعيدوكم في ملتهم ولن تفلحوا إذا أبداً [الكهف: 20] أي ما داموا في ملتهم، ألا ترى أنهم إذا لم يفعلوا أفلحوا كذا هذا؛ في البدائع. وقد بحث المحقق ابن الهمام في فتح القدير بأنه لما لم تمكن الحقيقة المجاز كان له مجازان: أحدهما ما ذكرتم من إرادة من بينهما تلاعن قائم حكماً. والثاني من وجد بينهما تلاعن في الخارج. وعلى هذا التقدير لا يجتمعان بعد الإكذاب بينهما إذ ارتفاع حكمه لا يوجب ارتفاع كونه قد تحقق له وجود في الخارج ولكن في النظر في أي الاحتمالين أرجح وأظن أن الثاني أسرع إلى الفهم ا هـ.
So the result is that the separation by li'an removes the marital bond and necessitates prohibition of cohabitation and marriage as long as they remain in the state of li'an. If one of them admits his/her lying, marriage and cohabitation become permissible according to Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf said: It necessitates a permanent prohibition like the prohibition of fosterage ... and affinity, because of the Prophet's saying: 'The two who perform li'an will never be together again.' And his statement implies that the separation is not dependent on a court ruling, as indicated in Fath al-Qadir. Their (the two disciples) evidence is that 'Uwaymir divorced his wife three times, so it became the sunnah of those who perform li'an that he must divorce her; if he does not, the judge acts in his stead, as in the case of the impotent man. Thus the separation is a divorce. As for the hadith, it cannot be taken literally because the literal meaning of 'mutala'inian' (the two who are engaged in li'an) is those who are actively occupied with the act; when they finish, the literal meaning no longer holds. So the intended meaning is the legal effect: that the ruling of li'an remains. After admission, its ruling no longer remains because it is invalidated. Thus neither the literal meaning nor the ruling remains, so they can be together. An analogy is the verse in the story of the People of the Cave: 'They will stone you or force you back to their religion, and you will never succeed then, ever' [18:20] meaning as long as they are in their religion. Don't you see that if they do not do that, they succeed? Similarly here; in al-Bada'i'. And the investigator Ibn al-Humam in Fath al-Qadir discussed that since the literal meaning could not be taken, there are two metaphorical interpretations: one is what you mentioned, meaning those between whom there is a legal effect of li'an. The second is those for whom li'an has occurred externally. According to this, they cannot be together after admission because the removal of the ruling does not necessitate removal of the fact that it occurred externally. But there is consideration as to which probability is more preponderant, and I think the second is quicker to understand.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses a key intra-Hanafi dispute: the nature of the separation caused by li'an. According to Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad (the third), the separation is not a divorce but a temporary prohibition that ends if either spouse admits lying. Abu Yusuf holds it is a permanent prohibition like that of fosterage, invoking the hadith 'the two who perform li'an will never be together again'. The author of al-Hidayah explains that the hadith cannot be taken literally because 'mutala'inian' refers to the act of li'an being ongoing, not after it ends. Therefore, after admission, since the legal effect of li'an disappears, the prohibition disappears. This is analogized to the Quranic verse about the People of the Cave where 'never' means as long as they persist in their religion. The commentator Ibn al-Humam notes that even metaphorically, there are two possible interpretations of the hadith, and he prefers the one that the prohibition only lasts as long as the li'an ruling exists.

Key Terms
الفرقة باللعان (al-furqa bil-li'an): The separation between spouses resulting from completing li'an.
حرمة مؤبدة (hurma mu'abbada): A permanent prohibition that prevents remarriage forever, akin to the prohibition due to fosterage or affinity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, when does the separation caused by li'an become permanently prohibitive?
A) Never, it can always be ended by admission
B) Only if the wife does not admit
C) Immediately after li'an, regardless of admission
D) Only if the husband divorces thrice

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the separation of li'an is a temporary prohibition that ends if either spouse admits to lying, allowing remarriage.

Li'an Not Valid for Accusing a Mute Person
Stating that li'an is not valid when the spouse being accused is mute because the essential pillar of pronouncing the testimony is missing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on li'an when the spouse is mute and explain why the verbal utterance of testimonies is a necessary pillar.

Core Idea
Li'an requires both spouses to verbally utter the testimonies; a mute person cannot fulfill this pillar.

Li'an is a special procedure in Islamic law where spouses exchange oaths to resolve accusations of adultery. However, this procedure has strict conditions. One key condition is that both spouses must be able to speak and pronounce the testimonies aloud. The following passage explains why li'an cannot take place when the accused spouse is mute, highlighting the essential role of verbal testimony.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the opening statement: 'لا لعان بقذف الأخرس' — there is no li'an when accusing a mute person. This is a clear ruling of invalidity (batil).
2
Identify the missing pillar (rukn)
The text states that the pillar (rukn) is missing: 'لفقد الركن منه وهو التلفظ بالشهادات' — because the pillar of li'an, which is uttering the testimonies, is absent.
The rukn of li'an is the verbal declaration 'ashhadu' from both parties.
3
Apply the reasoning to related cases
If the husband says 'I swear' instead of 'I testify', it is invalid because the specific wording of testimony is required. Similarly, if either spouse is mute, li'an is invalid.
The text suggests it would be better to explicitly state that li'an is invalid if both are mute or one is mute.
4
Note the analogy to divorce
The passage analogizes li'an to divorce: just as divorce cannot be effected by writing according to the Hanafi school, li'an also cannot be done through writing or signs.
This highlights the importance of oral expression in certain legal acts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا لعان بقذف الأخرس) لفقد الركن منه وهو التلفظ بالشهادات ولهذا لو قال أحلف مكان أشهد لا يجوز ولو قال ولا لعان إذا كانا أخرسين أو أحدهما لكان أولى للعلة المذكورة إذا كانت خرساء ولاحتمال تصديقها لو كانت ناطقة. وأشار إلى أنه لا يثبت بالكتابة كما لا يثبت الطلاق بها على المذهب.
(And there is no li'an by accusing a mute person) because the essential pillar [of li'an] is missing, which is uttering the testimonies. For this reason, if he says 'I swear' instead of 'I testify', it is not valid. And if he had said 'And there is no li'an if both are mute or one of them', it would have been more appropriate, because of the aforementioned reason if the woman is mute, and because of the possibility that she might testify if she were able to speak. And it indicates that li'an is not established by writing, just as divorce is not established by writing according to the madhhab.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq states a clear ruling: li'an cannot be performed when the accused spouse is mute. The reason is that li'an requires both parties to verbally pronounce the testimonies (ashhadu). The essential pillar (rukn) of li'an is verbal utterance. Therefore, if the husband says 'I swear' instead of 'I testify', it is invalid. Similarly, if either spouse is mute, li'an is not valid. The commentator suggests that the author could have explicitly included the case where both are mute or one is mute. The reason includes the possibility that if the mute spouse could speak, she might testify and thus li'an would be unnecessary. The text also notes that writing is not sufficient for li'an, just as it is not sufficient for divorce according to the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
الأخرس (al-akhras): A person who is mute and cannot speak.
التلفظ بالشهادات (al-talfuz bil-shahadat): Uttering the testimonies (ashhadu) aloud, which is a pillar of li'an.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the hadd punishment cannot be applied
B) Because li'an requires verbal testimony, which a mute cannot provide
C) Because the judge cannot confirm the accusation
D) Because the mute spouse cannot inherit

Key Takeaway
Li'an is invalid if either spouse is mute because the essential pillar of verbal testimony cannot be fulfilled.

Remarriage After Li'an: Conditions and Scenarios
Expansion on scenarios permitting remarriage after li'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a husband may remarry his wife after li'an and distinguish permissible from impermissible scenarios.

Core Idea
Remarriage after li'an becomes permissible when the basis of prohibition—imputation of unchastity—is removed by admission or actual zina.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the ruling that a husband can remarry his wife after li'an in several scenarios. The core principle is that the prohibition of remarriage is lifted when the wife's chastity is no longer intact (by her actual zina) or when the husband admits he lied. The commentator clarifies that the permissibility applies only to cases where the hadd punishment is flogging (before consummation), not stoning (after consummation).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and default ruling
After li'an and court-ordered separation, the husband is normally prohibited from remarrying the wife. The lesson explores when this prohibition is lifted.
Default: prohibition remains until a condition is met.
2
Recognize the primary condition: admission
If either spouse admits lying (ikdhab), the prohibition ends. The husband may remarry her whether or not he received the hadd punishment.
The text says 'if he admits his lying' – absolute, covering both punished and unpunished.
3
Recognize secondary condition: actual zina by wife
If the wife commits actual unlawful intercourse (zina) before consummation, she is flogged and then becomes permissible for remarriage. The term 'zina' here includes intercourse that may not be legally zina (e.g., by a non-Muslim) because it destroys chastity.
After consummation, the hadd is stoning, so this scenario does not apply.
4
Recognize tertiary condition: husband accuses another
If the husband accuses another woman of zina and is punished (flogged), he may also remarry his li'an wife. This shows the prohibition is lifted when the husband loses his own capacity for li'an.
The commentator suggests a broader phrasing would have been better.
5
Note the limitation: post-consummation zina
If the wife commits zina after consummation, she is stoned to death, so remarriage is inconceivable. The ruling only applies when the hadd is flogging (i.e., li'an before consummation).
This is a crucial boundary: the wife must survive the punishment.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وله أن ينكحها أي للملاعن بعد التفريق أن يتزوجها إذا أكذب نفسه . أطلقه فشمل ما إذا حد أو لم يحد فتقييد الشارح الحل بالحد اتفاقي، وكذا إذا أكذبت نفسها فصدقته. ... قوله : (وكذا إذا قذف غيرها فحد أو زنت فحدت يعني له أن ينكحها أيضاً إذا خرجا أو أحدهما عن أهلية اللعان أطلقه فشمل ما إذا خرسا أو أحدهما وأراد بالزنا الوطء الحرام وإن لم يكن زنا شرعياً ـ كما ذكره الاسبيجابي - لزوال عفتها ولو قال وكذا إن قذف أحدهما فحد لكان أولى لشموله المتلاعنين ولو أسقط قوله «فحد» لكان أولى لأن بمجرد زناها حلت له سواء حدت بأن وقع اللعان قبل الدخول ثم زنت فجلدت أو لم تجد لزوال العفة. وإنما قيدنا بهذه الصورة لأنه لو كان بعد الدخول كان حدها الرجم وهو إهلاك فلا يتصور القول بحلها بعده واستغنى بها عن تغيير الرواية بأنها زنت بالتشديد أي نسبت غيرها للزنا لمخالفته للرواية لأنها بتخفيف النون.
His statement: '(And he may marry her) means for the husband [who performed li'an] after the separation, he may marry her if he admits his lying. He made it absolute, so it includes whether he received hadd or not. So the commentator's restriction of permissibility to [the case of] hadd is incidental. Similarly, if she admits her lying and he believes her. ... His statement: 'And likewise if he accuses another and is punished, or she commits zina and is punished,' means he may also marry her if both or one of them lose the capacity for li'an. He made it absolute, so it includes if both are mute or one of them. And by 'zina' he means unlawful intercourse even if not legally zina, as mentioned by al-Isbijabi, because of the loss of her chastity. And if he had said 'And likewise if one of them accuses and is punished,' it would have been better because it includes the two who performed li'an. And if he had dropped the word 'in punished' it would have been better, because merely her committing zina makes her permissible for him, whether she was punished—as in li'an before consummation then she commits zina and is flogged—or not punished, due to loss of chastity. We only restrict to this scenario because if it were after consummation, her punishment would be stoning, which is destruction, so it is inconceivable to say she becomes permissible after that. And this suffices without changing the narration that {zannat} (with tashdid) meaning she attributed zina to another, because it contradicts the narration which has {zanat} (with tashdid [sic] actually with tashdid? unclear] with light nun.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the ruling that the husband can remarry his wife after li'an in several scenarios. The core rule: if either spouse admits lying (ikdhab), the prohibition ends and they can remarry, regardless of whether the husband received the hadd punishment. Additionally, if the wife commits actual zina (unlawful intercourse) and is punished, or if the husband accuses another woman and is punished, the prohibition also lifts. The commentator notes that the author could have made the text more inclusive. Importantly, if the wife committed zina after consummation (which would entail stoning), the husband cannot remarry her because she is executed. The ruling only applies to cases where the hadd is flogging (e.g., li'an before consummation). The passage also notes that the term 'zina' here refers to actual illicit intercourse, not merely accusation, and it includes intercourse that may not be legally zina (e.g., by a non-Muslim) because the effect is loss of chastity.

Key Terms
الزنا (al-zina): Unlawful sexual intercourse; here includes actual intercourse even if not technically zina (e.g., by a non-Muslim) because it affects chastity.
العفة (al-'iffa): Chastity; its loss (by committing zina) is the basis for lifting the prohibition after li'an.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the lesson, in which scenario does the husband NOT become permissible to remarry his wife after li'an?
A) The wife admits lying and the husband believes her.
B) The wife commits zina after li'an but before consummation and is flogged.
C) The wife commits zina after consummation (and would be stoned).
D) The husband accuses another woman of zina and is flogged.

Key Takeaway
Remarriage after li'an is permissible when the cause of the prohibition (the accusation of unchastity) is removed by admission or actual zina, but only when the wife survives the punishment (i.e., before consummation).

The Nature of Separation in Li'an: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on separation nature in li'an

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the nature of separation after li'an and explain the evidence for each view.

Core Idea
In Hanafi fiqh, li'an separation is automatic according to Abu Hanifa, but the Sahibayn treat it as a divorce requiring a judicial decree.

This lesson examines an important intra-Hanafi debate regarding the legal nature of the separation (furqa) that occurs after the mutual imprecation (li'an) between spouses. The core question is whether the separation happens automatically once the li'an process is complete, or whether it requires a formal divorce pronouncement by the husband or a judicial decree. The disagreement is rooted in different interpretations of the primary texts and analogies. Understanding this dispute will sharpen your ability to evaluate ikhtilaf within a single madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The question is: once li'an (mutual imprecation) is completed, does the separation occur automatically, or does it require additional action (divorce or judicial decree)?
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa holds that separation is automatic based on the hadith 'al-mutala'inan la yajtami'an abadan' (the two who imprecate will never be together again). He interprets 'never' as an absolute and immediate prohibition, implying the separation is not dependent on a judicial decree.
The hadith is considered a dalil naqli.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their proof
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad argue that the separation is a divorce (talaq) based on the precedent of 'Uwaymir al-'Ajlani, who divorced his wife three times after li'an. They analogize to the case of an impotent husband: if the husband fails to divorce, the judge does so in his place.
This is an analogy (qiyas) combined with a sunnah precedent.
4
Analyze the linguistic rebuttal regarding the hadith
The author refutes the Sahibayn's literal interpretation. The word 'al-mutafa'il' refers to the act of engaging in li'an; once the act is complete, the literal meaning ceases. The intended meaning is the ongoing legal effect. After the accusation of lying (ikdhab), the legal effect vanishes, so the prohibition on being together no longer applies. The analogy with the Quranic verse about the People of the Cave supports this metaphorical interpretation.
This is a linguistic analysis using usul al-fiqh principles (haqiqa vs. majaz).
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school is that of Abu Hanifa: the separation occurs automatically upon completion of li'an, without need for a divorce or judicial decree.
This is supported by the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq and later Hanafi authorities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لقوله عليه السلام والمتلاعنان لا يجتمعان أبداً ويقتضي قوله أن الفرقة لا تتوقف على القضاء كما أشار إليه في فتح القدير. ولهما أن عويمراً طلق الملاعنة ثلاثاً فصار سنة المتلاعنين لأنه يجب عليه أن يطلقها فإن لم يفعل ناب القاضي منابه كما في العنين فكانت الفرقة طلاقاً، وأما الحديث فلا يمكن العمل بحقيقته لأن حقيقة المتفاعل المتشاغل بالفعل ولما فرغا منه زالت الحقيقة فانصرف المراد إلى الحكم وهو أن يكون حكمه باقياً وبعد الإكذاب لم يبق حكمه لبطلانه فلم يبق حقيقة ولا حكماً فجاز اجتماعهما ونظيره قوله تعالى في قصة أصحاب الكهف إنهم إن يظهروا عليكم يرجموكم أو يعيدوكم في ملتهم ولن تفلحوا إذا أبداً [الكهف: ۲۰] أي ما داموا في ملتهم، ألا ترى أنهم إذا لم يفعلوا أفلحوا كذا هذا؛ في البدائع. وقد بحث المحقق ابن الهمام في فتح القدير بأنه لما لم تمكن الحقيقة المجاز كان له مجازان: أحدهما ما ذكرتم من إرادة من بينهما تلاعن قائم حكماً. والثاني من وجد بينهما تلاعن في الخارج. وعلى هذا التقدير لا يجتمعان بعد الإكذاب بينهما إذ ارتفاع حكمه لا يوجب ارتفاع كونه قد تحقق له وجود في الخارج ولكن في النظر في أي الاحتمالين أرجح وأظن أن الثاني أسرع إلى الفهم ا هـ .
Due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'The two who imprecate each other will never be together again' and his saying implies that the separation is not dependent on judicial decree, as indicated in Fath al-Qadir. And for them (the Sahibayn) is that 'Uwaymir divorced the imprecated woman three times, so it became the sunnah for those who imprecate that he must divorce her; if he does not, the judge acts in his stead like in the case of the impotent, so the separation is a divorce. As for the hadith, it cannot be taken literally, because the literal meaning of 'al-mutafa'il' is the one engaged in the action, and when they finish it, the literal meaning ceases; so the intent turns to the ruling, which is that its ruling remains. After the accusation of lying, its ruling does not remain due to its invalidity, so neither literal meaning nor ruling remains, so their being together is permissible. Its analogy is the saying of Allah Almighty in the story of the People of the Cave: 'Indeed, if they come to know of you, they will stone you or return you to their religion, and never will you succeed then, ever' [18:20], i.e., as long as they remain in their religion. Do you not see that if they do not do so, they succeed? Similarly here. [This is] in al-Bada'i'. And the investigator Ibn al-Humam discussed in Fath al-Qadir that since the literal meaning is not possible, metaphor had two possibilities: one is what you mentioned of intending the one between whom li'an exists in terms of ruling; the second is the one between whom li'an has occurred externally. On this basis, they do not meet after the accusation of lying because the lifting of its ruling does not necessitate the lifting of its having occurred externally. But one must consider which of the two probabilities is more likely, and I think the second is quicker to understand, end quote.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the legal nature of the separation (furqa) that occurs after li'an (mutual imprecation). The base text from Kanz al-Daqa'iq states that the separation does not depend on a judicial decree, based on the hadith 'al-mutala'inan la yajtami'an abadan' (the two who imprecate will never be together again). The commentator, al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, explains that this hadith implies automatic separation. However, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn) disagree, citing the precedent of 'Uwaymir al-'Ajlani, who divorced his wife after li'an. They argue that the husband must divorce his wife, and if he fails, the judge steps in—similar to the case of an impotent husband. Thus, the separation is a divorce (talaq), not an automatic annulment. The author then refutes the Sahibayn's literal interpretation of the hadith, explaining that the word 'al-mutafa'il' (the one who engages in the action) refers to the act of li'an itself; once the act is complete, the literal meaning ceases, and the intended meaning is the ongoing legal effect. After the accusation of lying (ikdhab), the legal effect also vanishes, so the prohibition on their being together no longer applies. The author draws an analogy with the Quranic verse about the People of the Cave. Ibn al-Humam adds that since the literal meaning is impossible, two metaphorical interpretations exist: either the hadith refers to those whose li'an is still legally active, or to those who have actually performed li'an in the past. He leans toward the second interpretation, which allows them to remarry after the li'an is invalidated. The mufta bihi position is that of Abu Hanifa: the separation occurs automatically upon completion of li'an, without need for a divorce or judicial decree.

Key Terms
المتلاعنان (al-mutala'inan): Two spouses who have performed li'an (mutual imprecation)
الفرقة (al-furqa): The permanent separation that occurs after li'an
حقيقة (haqiqa): Literal meaning of a word or phrase
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical meaning

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), what is the legal nature of the separation after li'an?
A) Automatic annulment without need for divorce.
B) A divorce that the husband must pronounce; if not, the judge acts.
C) A simple prohibition from living together that can be lifted by repentance.
D) The same as the separation in zihar.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's relied-upon position is that li'an leads to automatic separation without need for divorce, as held by Abu Hanifa.

Negating Pregnancy in Li'an: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on li'an for negating pregnancy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi intra-school dispute on using li'an to negate pregnancy and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Li'an for pregnancy is invalid because certainty is lacking; even with certainty, Abu Hanifa rejects it due to conditional accusation.

The husband may deny paternity of a child through li'an, but what if the wife is merely pregnant at the time of accusation? The Hanafi school splits: the Sahibayn allow li'an if the birth occurs within six months (proving the pregnancy existed), while Abu Hanifa rules it invalid unconditionally. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind each view and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi default ruling
Identify the general rule: li'an cannot negate pregnancy because at the moment of accusation, the existence of pregnancy is not certain (could be swelling).
This is the starting position of all Hanafis.
2
Identify the Sahibayn's exception
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad allow li'an if the wife gives birth in less than six months, because this proves the pregnancy existed at the time of accusation.
Six months is the minimum gestation period; birth earlier confirms conception before the accusation.
3
Follow Abu Hanifa's response
Even with certainty, Abu Hanifa rejects li'an because the husband's statement becomes conditional ('If you are pregnant, it is not from me'), and qadhf (accusation of zina) cannot be conditional.
Conditionality invalidates the legal effect of li'an.
4
Analyze why certainty does not override the conditionality rule
Understand that the prohibition on conditional qadhf is a strict rule; even certainty of pregnancy does not make the accusation unconditional.
The ruling is based on the wording, not just the underlying fact.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq sides with Abu Hanifa, indicating his view is the relied-upon position for fatwa.
Thus, the operative ruling is that li'an for negating pregnancy is invalid in all cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا ينفى الحمل لأنه لا يتيقن بقيامه عند القذف لاحتمال أنه انتفاخ ولو تيقنا بقيامه وقته بأن ولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر صار كأنه قال إن كنت حاملاً فحملك ليس مني والقذف لا يصح تعليقه بالشرط وهذا قول الإمام، وعندهما يجري اللعان إذا جاءت به لأقل من ستة أشهر للتيقن بقيامه وجوابه ما مر.
His statement: 'And pregnancy is not negated because its existence at the time of accusation is not certain due to the possibility that it is a swelling. But if we were certain of its existence at that time, as when she gives birth in less than six months, then it becomes as if he said: If you are pregnant, your pregnancy is not from me. And accusation (qadhf) is not valid when suspended on a condition. This is the statement of the Imam (Abu Hanifa). And according to them (the Sahibayn), li'an is carried out when she gives birth in less than six months due to certainty of its existence. And the answer is as previously mentioned.'
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on negating pregnancy (nafy al-haml) in li'an. The author explains that pregnancy cannot be negated by li'an because at the time of the accusation, the existence of pregnancy is not certain—it might be a mere swelling (intifakh). Therefore, li'an is not valid for negating pregnancy. However, if the wife gives birth less than six months after the accusation, the pregnancy is certain to have existed at the time of accusation. In that case, Abu Hanifa (the Imam) rules that the husband cannot use li'an to negate the pregnancy because his statement would be conditional ('If you are pregnant, it is not from me'), and qadhf (accusation of zina) cannot be made conditional. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: they hold that li'an is valid in this scenario because the certainty of pregnancy allows it. The text indicates that the author sides with Abu Hanifa, referring to 'the answer as previously mentioned' (referring to the earlier refutation). The key legal point is that li'an requires a direct, unconditional accusation of zina, and negating pregnancy is an indirect way of doing so. When the pregnancy is uncertain, li'an is invalid; when certain, Abu Hanifa still rejects li'an due to the conditionality, while the Sahibayn accept it.

Key Terms
نفي الحمل (nafy al-haml): The husband's denial of paternity of the pregnancy using li'an
التيقن (al-tayaqqu): Legal certainty that the pregnancy existed at the time of accusation
القذف (al-qadhf): Accusation of zina; in li'an context, the husband accuses his wife

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because pregnancy cannot be negated at all.
B) Because the accusation would be conditional, and qadhf cannot be conditional.
C) Because the child would be illegitimate.
D) Because li'an is only for a living child.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, li'an cannot be used to negate pregnancy because certainty is lacking; even when certain, the conditional nature of the accusation invalidates it, following Abu Hanifa's view.

Li'an: Denial of Pregnancy and Twin Issues
Expansion of li'an rulings on pregnancy and twin denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why denying pregnancy is invalid in Hanafi fiqh, contrast it with denying a child, and apply the ruling to twin scenarios to determine the consequence (hadd or li'an).

Core Idea
Pregnancy cannot be denied due to uncertainty; only the child can be denied, and contradictory twin denial triggers hadd.

This lesson continues the discussion of li'an, focusing on the critical distinction between denying pregnancy itself (invalid) and denying a child after birth (valid only under certain conditions). The text also addresses the case of twins and the legal effects that depend on birth, such as inheritance and maintenance. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly applying li'an rulings.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish denial of pregnancy from denial of child
Identify the key ruling: pregnancy cannot be denied because its existence at the time of defamation is uncertain (it could be swelling). Denial of the child is possible only after birth and under specific conditions (e.g., before congratulations or buying delivery tools).
The text states 'ولا ينفى الحمل' and the reason is uncertainty.
2
Apply the twin scenario
Work through the two cases: (1) Husband denies first twin but acknowledges second → he is flogged (hadd) for contradictory defamation. (2) He denies second but acknowledges first → he must perform li'an and both children belong to him.
The text says 'وإن نفي أول التوأمين وأقر بالثاني حد وإن عكس لاعن وثبت نسبهما'.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi dispute on conditional denial
Identify the disagreement between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding denying pregnancy. Imam holds that conditional defamation (e.g., 'If you are pregnant, it's not from me') is invalid. Sahibayn allow li'an if birth occurs within six months, establishing certainty.
Refer to the passage: 'وهذا قول الإمام، وعندهما يجري اللعان إذا جاءت به لأقل من ستة أشهر'.
4
Identify legal effects dependent on birth
List the rulings that only take effect after birth: inheritance, bequest, manumission, return of defective goods, and maintenance. The fetus is not a legal person until born.
The text covers these under 'وأما الإرث والوصية فيتوقفان على الولادة'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منه ولم ينف الحمل ولو نفي الولد عند التهنئة وابتياع آلة الولادة صح وبعده لا ولاعن فيهما وإن نفي أول التوأمين وأقر بالثاني حد وإن عكس لاعن وثبت نسبهما فيهما . يثبت بإشارة الأخرس للشبهة، وإلى أنه لو خرس أحدهما بعد اللعان وقبل التفريق فلا تفريق ولا حد كما لو ارتد أو أكذب نفسه قوله : (ولا ينفى الحمل لأنه لا يتيقن بقيامه عند القذف لاحتمال أنه انتفاخ ولو تيقنا بقيامه وقته بأن ولدت لأقل من ستة أشهر صار كأنه قال إن كنت حاملاً فحملك ليس مني والقذف لا يصح تعليقه بالشرط وهذا قول الإمام، وعندهما يجري اللعان إذا جاءت به لأقل من ستة أشهر للتيقن بقيامه وجوابه ما مر. وأما الإرث والوصية فيتوقفان على الولادة فيثبتان للولد لا للحمل، وأما عتقه فكذلك لقبوله التعليق بالشرط، وأما رد المبيعة بعيب الحمل فلأن الحمل ظاهر واحتمال الريح شبهة والرد بالعيب لا يمتنع بالشبهة، وكذا النسب يثبت مع الشبهة، وأما وجوب النفقة للمطلقة إذا ادعت حملاً فلقبول قولها في أمر عدتها والحق أن قول صاحب الهداية إن الأحكام لا تترتب عليه قبلها يراد به كل الأحكام وإنما يراد به بعضها كما في العناية وقد كتبنا في القواعد الفقهية مسائل أخرى تترتب عليه قبلها قوله : ( وتلاعنا بزنيت وهذا الحمل منه ولم ينف الحمل) لوجود القذف بصريح الزنا ونفي الحمل غير صحيح لأن قطع النسب حكم عليه ولا تترتب الأحكام عليه ولا له قبل الانفصال قوله : (ولو نفي الولد عند التهنئة وابتياع آلة الولادة صح وبعده لا ولا عن فيهما أي فيما إذا صح نفيه أو لم يصح لوجود القذف فيهما. والتهنئة هنأته بالولد بالتثقيل والهمز ؛ كذا في المصباح. فالتفصيل المذكور بين أن تقوم دلالة على إقراره بالولد أولا إنما هو في صحة النفي وعدمه لا في اللعان كما في المتون والشروح، وبه علم أن ما ذكره الولوالجي من أن اللعان إنما يجري إذا نفي بعد الولادة في مدة قصيرة أما بعد مدة طويلة فلا يصح سهو، ودل كلامه على أنه لو أقر صريحاً بالولد ثم يصح بالأولى كما قدمناه ولم يقدر مدة الولادة بوقت وهو ظاهر الرواية وقد قالوا : إن الإقرار بالولد الذي ليس منه حرام كالسكوت لاستلحاق نسب من ليس منه، وقد ذكر المصنف تبعاً للهداية شيئين قبول التهنئة وشراء آلة الولادة وزاد في الاختيار ثالثاً أن يقبل هدية الأهل فهي ثلاث لا يصح نفيه بعد واحدة منها والحق أنها أربع والرابع سكوته حتى مضى وقت التهنئة وشراء الآلة وهي ثلاثة أيام في رواية وسبعة في أخرى كما في الكافي. وقبول التهنئة ذكر ما يدل على القبول مثل أحسن الله بارك الله جزاك الله ، رزقك الله مثله أو أمن على دعاء المهنئ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. ولو كان غائباً لم يعلم بالولادة تعتبر المدة بعد قدومه . بالهمز من نفاه لا ونحوه فليحرر بالنقل ا هـ كذا في حواشي مسكين لأبي السعود وفيه نظر، فإن قول المصنف «أو زنت فحدت معناه له أن يتزوجها إذا زنت فحدت أي بعد الحد ولا يخفى أن الحد لو كان الرجم لا يتم إلا بموتها كما أفاده المؤلف بقوله وهو إهلاك فلا يتصور القول بحلها بعده. قوله : (وهي ثلاثة أيام في رواية (الخ ذكر في الفتح أنه لم يقدر لها مقدار في ظاهر الرواية وأن ما هنا ضعفه السرخسي بأن نصب المقادير بالرأي متعذر.
From it: and he does not deny the pregnancy. If he denies the child at the congratulations and buying the delivery tool, it is valid, but after that no, and li'an is performed in both. If he denies the first twin and acknowledges the second, he is flogged. If he reverses, he performs li'an and the lineage of both is established. It is established by the gesture of the mute due to the doubt. And that if one of them becomes mute after li'an and before separation, then no separation and no hadd, as if he apostatized or retracted his own statement. He said: (And pregnancy is not denied because its existence at the time of defamation is not certain due to the possibility that it is swelling. If we were certain of its existence at that time, as in she gives birth in less than six months, it becomes as if he said: If you are pregnant, then your pregnancy is not from me, and defamation cannot be suspended on a condition. This is the saying of Imam. According to them, li'an occurs if she gives birth in less than six months due to certainty of its existence, and its answer is what passed. As for inheritance and bequest, they depend on birth, so they are established for the child, not for the fetus. As for his manumission, likewise because it accepts suspension on a condition. As for returning the sold item due to the defect of pregnancy, because pregnancy is apparent and the possibility of wind is a doubt, and return due to defect is not prevented by doubt. Similarly, lineage is established with doubt. As for the obligation of maintenance for the divorced woman if she claims pregnancy, it is because her statement is accepted in the matter of her 'idda. The truth is that the statement of the author of Al-Hidayah that rulings are not based on it before her means all rulings, but rather some of them, as in Al-'Inaya, and we have written in the Fiqh principles other issues that are based on it before her. He said: (And they perform li'an due to fornication and this pregnancy is from him, and he does not deny the pregnancy) due to the existence of defamation by explicit fornication, and denial of pregnancy is not valid because cutting off lineage is a ruling upon it, and rulings are not based on it before separation. He said: (If he denies the child at the congratulations and buying the delivery tool, it is valid, but after that no, and li'an is performed in both) meaning whether his denial is valid or not due to the existence of defamation in both. Congratulations: he congratulated him on the child with tathqil and hamz; as in Al-Misbah. The detailed distinction between whether there is evidence of his acknowledgment of the child or not is only regarding the validity of denial and its absence, not regarding li'an, as in the texts and commentaries. And by it, it is known that what Al-Walwaji mentioned that li'an occurs only if he denies after birth in a short period, but after a long period it is not valid, is a mistake. And his words indicate that if he explicitly acknowledges the child, then it is valid a fortiori, as we have presented, and he did not set a time for birth, which is the apparent riwaya. And they said: Acknowledgment of a child who is not from him is forbidden, like silence, to establish lineage of one not from him. The author, following Al-Hidayah, mentioned two things: accepting congratulations and buying the delivery tool. And in Al-Ikhtiyar, a third is added: that he accepts the gift of the family, so there are three, and denial is not valid after any one of them. The truth is that there are four, and the fourth is his silence until the time of congratulations and buying the tool passes, which is three days in one riwaya and seven in another, as in Al-Kafi. Accepting congratulations includes what indicates acceptance, like 'Ahsana Allah, Baraka Allah, Jazaka Allah, Razaka Allah mithlahu' or saying 'Amin' to the congratulator's prayer, as in Fath al-Qadir. If he was absent and did not know of the birth, the period is considered from his arrival. With hamz from 'nafah'? No? And similar, let it be verified by transmission, end quote, as in the margins of Miskin by Abu al-Sa'ud. And in it, there is consideration, for the author's saying 'Or she committed fornication and was flogged' means he may marry her if she committed fornication and was flogged, i.e., after the hadd. And it is not hidden that if the hadd was stoning, it is only completed with her death, as the author expressed by saying 'it is destruction', so one cannot imagine the permissibility of marriage after it. He said: (And it is three days in one riwaya...) mentioned in Al-Fath that no amount was set in the apparent riwaya, and what is here was weakened by Al-Sarakhsi because setting amounts by opinion is impossible.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the chapter on li'an, focusing on the nuanced rulings regarding denial of pregnancy and twins. The commentator expands on the matn, clarifying that pregnancy cannot be denied because its existence at the time of defamation is uncertain (it could be swelling). However, if she gives birth within six months, certainty is established, and the denial becomes void. There is an intra-Hanafi dispute: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that li'an is not valid because the denial is conditional, while the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) allow li'an for certainty. The text then discusses various legal effects that depend on birth, such as inheritance, bequest, manumission, return of defective goods, and lineage. A detailed analysis follows regarding the timing of denial: if the husband accepts congratulations or buys delivery tools, his subsequent denial is invalid; silence for a few days also counts as acknowledgment. The passage ends with a marginal note criticizing the lack of a fixed time limit in the apparent riwaya, noting Al-Sarakhsi's objection.

Key Terms
لعان (li'an): Mutual imprecation between spouses when husband accuses wife of zina and denies paternity; involves swearing and cursing.
توأمان (taw'amān): Twins born from the same pregnancy; here, legal rulings apply to each twin individually.
حد (hadd): Fixed corporal punishment for certain crimes; here, flogging for false accusation (qadhf).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He is flogged for contradicting himself.
B) He must perform li'an.
C) Both children's lineage is denied.
D) No punishment because twins are considered one entity.

Key Takeaway
In li'an, pregnancy cannot be denied due to uncertainty; only the child can be denied before acknowledgment, and contradictory twin denial warrants hadd.

Definition of al-'Innin and the Wife's Right to Annulment
Defines 'innin and gives initial ruling on wife's khiyar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of an 'innin and identify the circumstances under which his wife has the option to annul the marriage.

Core Idea
An 'innin's inability or lack of desire gives his wife the right to annulment, but her consent forfeits that right.

This lesson introduces the legal definition of impotence ('inna) in Hanafi fiqh and the initial rulings concerning the wife's option (khiyar) to annul the marriage. The text from Al-Bahr al-Raiq defines an 'innin as a man who cannot have intercourse or does not desire women, and then notes that if the wife is satisfied with this condition, she loses her right to annulment—a point that is contested by later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Understand the linguistic and legal definition
Identify the Arabic term 'innin and its definition: a man who cannot have intercourse or does not desire women. Recognize that this definition is used by jurists.
The definition is both physical and psychological.
2
Recognize the legal consequence for marriage
Note that the husband's condition as 'innin triggers a special right for the wife: she may seek annulment (khiyar al-faskh) from a qadi.
This is a gender-specific right; the wife is not obliged to remain in an unconsummated marriage.
3
Identify the effect of the wife's satisfaction
According to the text, if the wife is satisfied with her husband despite his impotence, she loses her option to annul. This is presented as the standard position.
The phrase 'لو رضيت به فلا خيار لها' is quoted.
4
Note the scholarly dispute
Al-Ramli objects to this automatic loss of khiyar, suggesting that the wife's satisfaction does not necessarily extinguish her right. This indicates an intra-Hanafi disagreement.
The dispute is not resolved in the text.
5
Apply the ruling to a hypothetical
Consider a scenario: A wife discovers her husband is 'innin but tells the judge she is content. According to the standard Hanafi view, she cannot later seek annulment. If she is not satisfied, she may annul after one year (per general Hanafi position, though not stated here).
The one-year period is from other sources; this text does not mention a time limit.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب العنين وغيره يقال رجل عنين لا يقدر على إتيان النساء أو لا يشتهي النساء، وامرأة عنينة لا تشتهي الرجال، والفقهاء يقولون به عنة وفي كلام الجوهري ما يشبهه ولم أجده لغيره ولفظه : عن عن امرأته تعنيناً بالبناء للمفعول إذا حكم عليه القاضي بذلك أو منع عنها بالسحر، والاسم قوله : (وهي ثلاثة أيام في رواية (الخ ذكر في الفتح أنه لم يقدر لها مقدار في ظاهر الرواية وأن ما هنا ضعفه السرخسي بأن نصب المقادير بالرأي متعذر. باب العنين وغيره قوله : (لكن قولهم لو رضيت به فلا خيار لها ينافيه قال الرملي : هذا غير مسلم فإن ذلك لا
Chapter of the Impotent and Others. It is said: an impotent man is one who cannot have intercourse with women or does not desire women. An impotent woman is one who does not desire men. The jurists say: he has impotence. In the speech of Al-Jawhari, something similar, and I did not find it for others. Its wording: 'He turned from his wife, turning (passive voice) when the judge passed judgment on him or he was prevented from her by magic. And the noun. Commentary
This passage marks the beginning of the chapter on the impotent man ('innin) and related rulings. The definition is given linguistically and legally: a man who cannot perform intercourse or has no desire. The text also includes a marginal note that discusses a previous ruling about the time limit for denying a child (three days), which is actually a comment on the preceding chapter on li'an. This note criticizes the setting of specific days as arbitrary. Then the chapter repeats its heading and begins a discussion on whether the wife's consent eliminates her option to annul the marriage. Al-Ramli's opinion is mentioned but cut off. The commentary here is minimal as the text is fragmented.

Key Terms
عنين ('innin): A man who cannot have intercourse with women or does not desire women.

Key Takeaway

Three Scenarios of Twin Paternity Denial
Commentary on matn statements about twin paternity denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios of twin paternity denial in Hanafi fiqh and the legal consequence for each (hadd, li'an, or affirmation).

Core Idea
Denying one twin while acknowledging the other triggers hadd or li'an; denying both affirms their lineage.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq explains three situations when a father denies paternity of twin children. The ruling hinges on whether he acknowledges one twin or both. Understanding the logic of self-contradiction and the role of li'an is key to applying these rules.

Guided Path
1
Identify the father's statement
Determine which twin(s) he denies or acknowledges. The passage covers three patterns: deny first/acknowledge second, acknowledge first/deny second, deny both.
The father's exact wording matters: he must clearly state his denial or acknowledgment.
2
Apply the rule for denying first and acknowledging second
If he denies the first twin but acknowledges the second, he has contradicted himself. This is qadhf against the first twin, incurring hadd.
The hadd is 80 lashes for a free man, as in standard qadhf.
3
Apply the rule for acknowledging first and denying second
If he acknowledges the first and denies the second, he is a slanderer (qadif) against the second. Since he did not retract, he must perform li'an with his wife.
Li'an severs the marriage and establishes the child's lineage to the mother.
4
Apply the rule for denying both
If he denies both twins, his denial is rejected because twins are from one water. Both children's lineage to him is affirmed.
Also applies if one twin dies before li'an; the living twin's lineage is established.
5
Note the exception for retraction
If after acknowledging one and denying the other, he later says 'both are my sons' or 'neither is my son', the hadd is dropped.
Retraction before punishment removes the penalty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وإن نفى أول التوأمين وأقر بالثاني حد لأنه أكذب نفسه بدعوى الثاني. التوأم فوعل والأنثى توأمة والاثنان توأمان والجمع توائم وتوأم كدخان؛ كذا في المصباح قوله : (وإن عكس لاعن بأن أقر بالأول ونفى الثاني لأنه قاذف بنفي الثاني ولم يرجع عنه قوله : (وثبت نسبهما فيهما) أي في المسألتين لأنهما خلقا من ماء واحد، والتوأمان ولدان بين ولادتها أقل ستة أشهر، وفيه إشارة إلى أنه لو نفاهما ثم مات أحدهما قبل اللعان لزماه وقدمنا تفاريعه. ولو جاءت بثلاثة في بطن واحد فنفى الثاني وأقر بالأول والثالث يلاعن وهم بنوه، ولو نفى الأول والثالث وأقر بالثاني يحدوهم بنوه؛ كذا في شرح النقاية. اعلم أنه في صورة ما إذا أقر بالأول ونفى الثاني إذا قال بعده هما ابناي أو ليسا بابني فلا حد فيهما ؛ في فتح القدير. وفي شهادات الجامع للصدر الشهيد من باب شهادة ولد الملاعنة : باع أحد التوأمين وقد ولدا في ملكه وأعتقه المشتري فشهد لبائعه تقبل، فإن ادعى الباقي ثبت نسبهما وانتقض البيع والعتق والقضاء ويرد ما قبض أو مثله إن هلك للاستناد كتحويل العقد، وإن كان القضاء قصاصاً في طرف أو نفس فأرشه عليه دون العاقلة لأنه بدعواه. ثم اعلم أنه إذا نفى نسب التوأمين ثم مات أحدهما عن توأمه وأمه وأخ لأمه فالإرث أثلاث فرضاً ورداً للأم السدس وللأخوين الثلث والنصف بود عليهم. وهذا يبين أن قطع النسب يجري في التوأم لأنه لو لم يقطع نسبه عن أخيه التوأم لكان عصبة يأخذ الثلثين وقطع النسب عن الأخ التوأم بالتبعية لأبيهما وقد قدمناه عن الجامع وتمامه في شرح التلخيص من باب شهادة ولد الملاعنة والله سبحانه وتعالى أعلم بالصواب .
His saying: (And if he denies the first of the twins and acknowledges the second, he is subject to the hadd penalty because he has belied himself by claiming the second. 'Taw'am' is a pattern 'faw'al', the female is 'taw'amah', the two are 'taw'aman', and the plural is 'tawa'imu' and 'taw'am' like 'dukhan'; as stated in al-Misbah. His saying: (And if he reverses, he performs li'an, by acknowledging the first and denying the second, because he is a slanderer by denying the second and did not retract. His saying: (And the lineage of both is affirmed in both) means in both cases, because they were created from one water, and twins are two children born with less than six months between their births. And there is an indication that if he denied both and then one died before li'an, his lineage is established. And we previously presented its branches. And if she gives birth to three at once, then he denies the second and acknowledges the first and third, he performs li'an and they are his sons. And if he denies the first and third and acknowledges the second, he is subject to hadd and they are his sons; as in Sharh al-Niqayah. Know that in the case where he acknowledges the first and denies the second, if he then says 'both are my sons' or 'both are not my sons', there is no hadd; in Fath al-Qadir. And in the chapter on the testimony of the child of li'an from al-Jami' of al-Sadr al-Shahid: One of the twins was sold, and they were born in his ownership, and the purchaser freed him, and he testified for his seller, it is accepted. If the remaining twin claims (lineage), the lineage of both is established, and the sale and manumission and judgment are overturned, and what was received is returned or its equivalent if destroyed, due to reliance like shifting the contract. And if the judgment was retribution for a limb or life, then the blood money is upon him, not upon the 'aqila, because of his claim. Then know that if he denies the lineage of the twins and then one dies, leaving his twin, his mother, and a maternal brother, the inheritance is into thirds by prescribed shares and return: for the mother one-sixth, and for the two brothers one-third, and the half is upon them by way of substitution. And this shows that cutting off lineage applies to the twin, because if his lineage was not cut off from his twin brother, he would be an 'asaba taking two-thirds. And the cutting of lineage from the twin brother is by way of following their father. And we have previously stated it from al-Jami' and its completion is in Sharh al-Talkhis from the chapter on the testimony of the child of li'an. And Allah, glorified and exalted, knows best.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn's rulings on twin children and paternity denial. The commentator explains three scenarios: (1) If a man denies the first twin but acknowledges the second, he incurs the hadd of qadhf because he contradicts himself. (2) If he acknowledges the first and denies the second, he must perform li'an. (3) If he denies both, the lineage of both is affirmed. The discussion includes inheritance implications and the effect on sales and judgments, all within the Hanafi framework. The key is that denial of paternity for one twin does not sever the relationship between the twins; if he denies both, the twin relationship remains intact.

Key Terms
توأم (taw'am): A twin; a child born from the same pregnancy as another. The singular masculine is taw'am, feminine taw'amah, dual taw'aman, plural tawa'imu.
قاذف (qadif): One who commits qadhf, i.e., false accusation of unchastity, subject to the hadd penalty.
لعان (li'an): The mutual oath of condemnation between a husband and wife when the husband accuses his wife of adultery without witnesses.
نسب (nasab): Lineage, paternity; the legal relationship between a child and his father.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the consequence when a father acknowledges the first twin but denies the second?
A) He incurs the hadd of qadhf.
B) He must perform li'an.
C) The lineage of both twins is not established.
D) He must pay compensation.

Key Takeaway
The father's contradictory statements about twin children determine whether he incurs hadd, must perform li'an, or has his denial rejected entirely.

Inheritance and Sales Consequences of Twin Paternity Denial
Commentary on matn statements about twin paternity denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the inheritance distribution when one twin dies after paternity denial, and explain how the establishment of lineage affects prior sales and judgments.

Core Idea
Denying both twins cuts their lineage from the father but not from each other, affecting inheritance shares and invalidating prior transactions.

This passage extends the discussion to two important practical consequences: the inheritance of a deceased twin whose father denied paternity, and the effect on a sale of one twin when lineage is later established. Understanding these shows how deeply paternity rulings affect property and family law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the inheritance scenario
The father denies both twins. One twin dies, leaving his twin (full brother), mother, and a maternal brother.
The deceased has no father (denial cuts lineage from him), but the twin brother is still a full brother through the mother.
2
Determine the shares without the twin relationship
Since the father cut off the twin's lineage, the deceased's heir set includes: mother (fixed share 1/6), maternal brother (fixed share 1/6), and the full brother (twin) as 'asaba if not cut. But because lineage is cut, the twin is not a paternal relative, so he becomes a uterine brother? Actually the text says the mother gets 1/6, the two brothers (twin and maternal brother) get 1/3 equally, and the remaining half is returned to them by way of substitution.
The text explicitly says 'الإرث أثلاث فرضاً ورداً للأم السدس وللأخوين الثلث والنصف بود عليهم' – the inheritance is divided into thirds: mother 1/6, two brothers 1/3, and half is given to them by way of substitution (perhaps meaning the residual goes to them).
3
Understand the logic of the division
If the twin brother's lineage was not cut, he would be an 'asaba taking 2/3. But since it's cut, he is like a uterine brother sharing equally with the maternal brother. The mother takes her prescribed share, and the remaining is divided among the brothers with a return (radd) if needed.
This shows that cutting lineage does not eliminate the twin bond entirely; they are still brothers through the mother.
4
Analyze the sales scenario
A father sells one of his twin sons (who are his slaves? The text says 'إذا باع أحد التوأمين وقد ولدا في ملكه' – he sold one of the twins while they were born in his ownership, meaning he owned them as slaves). The purchaser frees him, and then the freed slave testifies for the seller.
The question: does the testimony stand? The text says it is accepted initially, but if the remaining twin later claims lineage, the sale and manumission are overturned.
5
Apply the ruling when lineage is later established
If the other twin comes forward and proves they are both sons of the father, then the sale of his brother was invalid because a free person cannot be sold. The judgment is overturned, and the buyer must return the slave or pay compensation.
If the judgment was for retaliation (qisas), the blood money is on the father (due to his claim) and not on the 'aqila.

His saying: (And if he denies the first of the twins and acknowledges the second, he is subject to the hadd penalty because he has belied himself by claiming the second. 'Taw'am' is a pattern 'faw'al', the female is 'taw'amah', the two are 'taw'aman', and the plural is 'tawa'imu' and 'taw'am' like 'dukhan'; as stated in al-Misbah. His saying: (And if he reverses, he performs li'an, by acknowledging the first and denying the second, because he is a slanderer by denying the second and did not retract. His saying: (And the lineage of both is affirmed in both) means in both cases, because they were created from one water, and twins are two children born with less than six months between their births. And there is an indication that if he denied both and then one died before li'an, his lineage is established. And we previously presented its branches. And if she gives birth to three at once, then he denies the second and acknowledges the first and third, he performs li'an and they are his sons. And if he denies the first and third and acknowledges the second, he is subject to hadd and they are his sons; as in Sharh al-Niqayah. Know that in the case where he acknowledges the first and denies the second, if he then says 'both are my sons' or 'both are not my sons', there is no hadd; in Fath al-Qadir. And in the chapter on the testimony of the child of li'an from al-Jami' of al-Sadr al-Shahid: One of the twins was sold, and they were born in his ownership, and the purchaser freed him, and he testified for his seller, it is accepted. If the remaining twin claims (lineage), the lineage of both is established, and the sale and manumission and judgment are overturned, and what was received is returned or its equivalent if destroyed, due to reliance like shifting the contract. And if the judgment was retribution for a limb or life, then the blood money is upon him, not upon the 'aqila, because of his claim. Then know that if he denies the lineage of the twins and then one dies, leaving his twin, his mother, and a maternal brother, the inheritance is into thirds by prescribed shares and return: for the mother one-sixth, and for the two brothers one-third, and the half is upon them by way of substitution. And this shows that cutting off lineage applies to the twin, because if his lineage was not cut off from his twin brother, he would be an 'asaba taking two-thirds. And the cutting of lineage from the twin brother is by way of following their father. And we have previously stated it from al-Jami' and its completion is in Sharh al-Talkhis from the chapter on the testimony of the child of li'an. And Allah, glorified and exalted, knows best.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn's rulings on twin children and paternity denial. The commentator explains three scenarios: (1) If a man denies the first twin but acknowledges the second, he incurs the hadd of qadhf because he contradicts himself. (2) If he acknowledges the first and denies the second, he must perform li'an. (3) If he denies both, the lineage of both is affirmed. The discussion includes inheritance implications and the effect on sales and judgments, all within the Hanafi framework. The key is that denial of paternity for one twin does not sever the relationship between the twins; if he denies both, the twin relationship remains intact.

Key Terms
توأم (taw'am): A twin; a child born from the same pregnancy as another.
نسب (nasab): Lineage, paternity.
عصبة (asabah): Male agnatic heir entitled to the residue after fixed shares.
رد (radd): Return of surplus shares to eligible heirs proportionally when no 'asaba exists.
بود (bud): Substitution; the method of distributing the surplus among the brothers in this case.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the share of the mother when one twin dies after the father has denied both twins?
A) 1/3
B) 1/6
C) 1/4
D) 1/8

Key Takeaway
Denying both twins severs their paternal lineage but not their bond as brothers through the mother, affecting inheritance and nullifying any prior sales.

Definition and Ruling on the 'Unnah and Majbub
Defining the impotent man and the castrated man with legal implications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definitions of 'unnah and majbub and identify the conditions under which the wife may seek annulment.

Core Idea
The wife's right to annulment depends on the husband's condition: no cure for majbub, hope for 'unnah.

This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq defines two categories of male sexual incapacity: 'unnah (impotence) and majbub (castration). The Hanafi school grants the wife a right to seek judicial annulment (faskh) if the husband falls into either category, provided she was unaware at marriage. The commentator also explores analogous cases in lease and sale to illustrate the principle that one cannot claim relief for a self-inflicted harm.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish 'unnah from majbub
Identify that 'unnah is a man who cannot penetrate despite a functioning organ, while majbub has had his genitals excised and is permanently incapable.
The majbub is considered completely hopeless, whereas 'unnah may have potential for cure.
2
Identify the wife's right
Recognise that the wife has the right (haqq) to demand annulment from the judge if the husband is 'unnah or majbub, but it is not automatic; she must request it.
3
Note the conditions for the right
Understand that the wife must have been unaware of the condition at marriage and must not have explicitly accepted it. If she knew and agreed, the option is lost.
Customary acceptance may also nullify the right, as per al-Ramli's commentary.
4
Examine the waiting period
Analyse the dispute: some narrations say three days, but al-Sarakhsi weakens this, and the relied-upon view gives no fixed period; the judge assesses based on custom.
This shows the Hanafi method of preferring custom over arbitrary numerical limits.
5
Compare analogous cases
See how the commentator draws parallels to a tenant damaging a house or a buyer destroying goods – where the right to remedy is lost if the harm is self-inflicted.
This reinforces the principle that one cannot claim relief for a condition one caused or accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب العنين وغيره يقال رجل عنين لا يقدر على إتيان النساء أو لا يشتهي النساء، وامرأة عنينة لا تشتهي الرجال، والفقهاء يقولون به عنة وفي كلام الجوهري ما يشبهه ولم أجده لغيره ولفظه : عن عن امرأته تعنيناً بالبناء للمفعول إذا حكم عليه القاضي بذلك أو منع عنها بالسحر، والاسم قوله : (وهي ثلاثة أيام في رواية (الخ ذكر في الفتح أنه لم يقدر لها مقدار في ظاهر الرواية وأن ما هنا ضعفه السرخسي بأن نصب المقادير بالرأي متعذر. باب العنين وغيره قوله : (لكن قولهم لو رضيت به فلا خيار لها ينافيه قال الرملي : هذا غير مسلم فإن ذلك لا يلزم منه رضاها ا هـ. وفيه تأمل فإنه وإن لم يلزم عقلاً لكنه لازم عادة كما لو تزوجته عالمة بحاله والوطء حقها وقد فوتته بضعها قوله : (أحدهما لو خرب المستأجر الدار قال الرملي : يعني ليس له فسخ الإجارة بهذا العيد لأنه هو الذي أحدثه وقوله لو أتلف البائع الخ يعني ليس له طلب الثمن لأنه هو الذي أبطل حقه فيه بإتلاف المبيع قوله : (من كان ذكره صغيراً كالزر) بكسر الزاي واحد الأزرار قوله : (لا من كانت آلته قصيرة الخ) بحث فيه الشرنبلالي في شرحه على الوهبانية فقال
Chapter on the impotent (al-'unan) and others. It is said: an impotent man is one who cannot have intercourse with women or does not desire women; and an impotent woman is one who does not desire men. The jurists say: 'he is afflicted with 'unnah'. And in the speech of al-Jawhari there is something similar, but I did not find it from others. His wording: 'He is turned away from his wife by enchantment' in the passive voice, if the judge has ruled that against him, or he is prevented from her by magic. And the noun... His saying: (And it is three days in a narration...). It is mentioned in al-Fath that no fixed period was determined in the zahir al-riwayah, and that what is here was criticized by al-Sarakhsi because setting durations by opinion is impossible. Chapter on the impotent and others. His saying: (But their saying: 'If she is satisfied with him, then there is no option for her' is contradictory.) Al-Ramli said: This is not accepted, because that does not necessarily entail her satisfaction. There is reflection in it, because although it is not logically necessary, it is customarily necessary, as if she married him knowing his condition. And intercourse is her right, and she has forfeited her vagina by it. His saying: (One of them: if the tenant damages the house...) Al-Ramli said: It means he does not have the right to void the lease due to this defect because he caused it. And his saying: if the seller destroys the goods... means he has no right to demand the price because he has nullified his right by destroying the sold item. His saying: (One whose private part is small like a button) with a kasra on the za', one of the buttons. His saying: (Not one whose tool is short...) Al-Shurunbulali discussed it in his commentary on al-Wahbaniyyah and said...
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage defines the types of impotence ('unnah) and related conditions like the majbub (castrated). The Hanafi jurists distinguish between the 'unnah who cannot achieve penetration at all despite a functioning organ (due to illness, etc.) and the majbub whose organ and testicles are removed. The wife has the option to seek annulment if the husband falls into these categories, provided she was unaware. The text also notes that mere satisfaction with the condition may nullify her option if it is customary. The commentator further compares to analogous cases in lease and sale where the aggrieved party may not claim relief if the harm is self-inflicted. The discussion reflects the Hanafi reliance on custom and legal maxims.

Key Terms
عنين ('unan): A man who cannot have intercourse with women or does not desire them; legally, one who cannot penetrate despite a functional organ, due to illness, magic, or weakness.
مجبوب (majbub): A man whose penis and testicles have been excised; he is considered completely incapable of intercourse.
عنة ('unnah): The condition of being impotent; the noun form used by jurists for the state of the 'unnah.
شكاز (shakkaz): A type of weak erection that resolves before full intercourse without re-arousal; considered a form of 'unnah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What distinguishes the majbub from the 'unnah in terms of the wife's right to annulment?
A) The wife of a majbub must wait a year.
B) The wife of a majbub can seek immediate annulment without waiting.
C) The majbub is given a three-day respite.
D) The wife has no right to annulment if the majbub was castrated before marriage.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school grants the wife a right to annulment if the husband is sexually incapable, with the critical distinction being permanence of the condition and the wife's prior knowledge.

Definitions and Basic Rulings for 'Aneen and Majbub
Commentary with definitions and primary rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the linguistic and juristic definitions of al-'aneen and al-majbub, and apply the primary Hanafi rulings on the wife's right to separation.

Core Idea
An 'aneen gets one year to recover; a majbub gets immediate separation—because hope of cure makes all the difference.

In this passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author begins by clarifying the proper linguistic and juristic definitions of al-'aneen (impotent man) before turning to the legal consequences. The central distinction is between the 'aneen, who may recover and is given a one-year deferment, and the majbub (castrated), whose condition is permanent, leading to an immediate right of separation for the wife. This lesson will anchor you in the core definitions and the foundational rulings that follow from them.

Guided Path
1
Linguistic definition of 'aneen
Understand the root meaning: the penis turns away (ya'unn) from the vulva.
Derived from 'al-‘ann' meaning turning aside.
2
Juristic definition of 'aneen
Note that inability to penetrate due to disease, even if partial or with erectile function, qualifies.
Includes cases where he can only penetrate some women.
3
Distinguish 'aneen from shakkaz and others
The shakkaz (premature ejaculator without subsequent erection) is treated like 'aneen.
The key is lack of full penetration.
4
State the ruling for 'aneen
A one-year deferment is given to allow for possible recovery before the wife can seek separation.
This is from the Hanafi school; Shafi'i disagrees.
5
State the ruling for majbub
Immediate separation is granted because there is no hope of cure.
Analogous cases: extremely short organ, complete inability to insert.
